\begin{itemize}

\]

Let's attempt to analyze the game more systematically. We can think of the game as a kind of "two-resource" game. Each player has a "budget" that must be satisfied at the end of their turn. For Alice, the budget for the sum is λ n at odd n; for Bazza, the budget for the sum of squares is n at even n.

We can consider the worst-case scenario for Alice: she wants to force a win; she will try to maximize T_n at odd turns. However, Bazza will try to minimize T_n by choosing small x_{2k} to keep T_{2k} ≤ 2k, but he might also try to increase S_{2k} to cause Alice to lose later. So we need to find a strategy for Alice that works regardless of Bazza's actions, and a strategy for Bazza that works regardless of Alice's actions.

This is reminiscent of a "pursuit" game where each player tries to push the opponent's "potential" beyond a bound. Perhaps we can model it as a "strategy stealing" or "minimax" where the outcome is determined by a "critical" sequence where both players play "optimally" to keep the game going.

Let's define the "optimal" play for each player given the other's moves. Since the game is infinite, we can think about the "value" of the state. The state after some turns is (S, T, n) where n is the number of turns already taken. But the constraints are given in terms of n; at odd n, Alice chooses x such that S + x ≤ λ n. At even n, Bazza chooses x such that T + x^2 ≤ n. After that, n increases by 1. So the state can be described by (S, T, parity of n). But the bounds also depend on n, not just parity.

We can attempt to find a necessary condition for Bazza to survive indefinitely. He must be able to choose x_{2k} such that T_{2k} ≤ 2k for all k. Since T_{2k} = T_{2k-1} + x_{2k}^2. Similarly, for Alice to survive, she must have S_{2k-1} ≤ λ(2k-1) for all k.

The game ends when a player cannot choose a nonnegative x satisfying their inequality.

Let's define for each turn n, the "score" that the player wants to keep. For Alice, she wants to keep T_n small? Actually, she wants T_n to be large to cause Bazza to lose, but she also wants to keep S_n small to avoid losing later. So she has a trade-off. Similarly, Bazza wants S_n large to cause Alice to lose, but he also wants T_n small to keep his square sum within bound.

Perhaps we can analyze the game as a "duel" where each player has a "budget" that can be spent on either linear sum or square sum, but the budgets are different. Actually, we can consider that the linear bound at odd n is λ n, and the square bound at even n is n. So the "cost" of a number x in terms of linear sum is x, and in terms of square sum is x^2. So each player, when choosing x, must ensure that after their turn, the cumulative linear sum (for Alice) is within her bound, and the cumulative square sum (for Bazza) is within his bound. The other player's cumulative sum is not directly constrained, but they will be constrained by the next turn.

We can think of the game as a kind of "resource allocation" where each player has a "budget" for their own sum, and they can allocate resources to increase the opponent's sum. For instance, when Bazza chooses x_{2k}, he can increase S_{2k} by x_{2k}, which then makes it harder for Alice to choose a large x_{2k+1} later. But he also must keep T_{2k} ≤ 2k, which limits how large x_{2k} can be. So he can only increase S by at most sqrt(2k - T_{2k-1}). Similarly, when Alice chooses x_{2k-1}, she can increase T by x_{2k-1}^2, which makes it harder for Bazza to have a large x_{2k} later, but she also must keep S_{2k-1} ≤ λ(2k-1), so she can increase T by at most (λ(2k-1) - S_{2k-2})^2.

Thus, the game can be seen as a "bidding" game where each player tries to increase the opponent's cumulative sum to exceed the bound for the next turn.

Let's attempt to find a "critical" sequence where both players play "optimally" to keep the game going as long as possible. That is, if the game can be drawn, then there exists a sequence of moves such that the survival conditions hold for all n. Conversely, if no such sequence exists, then one player can force a win. This is like the "maximin" value of the game.

Consider the following approach: Define a function f(k) = maximum possible S_{2k} that can be achieved while still satisfying all constraints up to that point, assuming both players play optimally to maximize S? But we want to see if Bazza can cause Alice to lose. So we need to see if there is a strategy for Bazza that ensures that for some k, S_{2k} > λ(2k+1). That would require that he can increase S_{2k} sufficiently, given that Alice will try to keep T_{2k-1} high to limit his increase. So we need to analyze the "best possible" S_{2k} that Bazza can force, assuming Alice plays to minimize his ability to increase S. Similarly, we need to analyze the "best possible" T_{2k-1} that Alice can force, assuming Bazza plays to minimize her ability to increase T.

This suggests we can define recurrence relations for the "minimal" T_{2k-1} that Alice can ensure (or maximal S_{2k} that Bazza can ensure). Let's attempt.

Suppose the game is at the start of turn n=2k-1 (odd), after Bazza's previous move at n=2k-2 (even). State: S_{2k-2} and T_{2k-2} are known. It's Alice's turn to choose x_{2k-1} (nonnegative) such that S_{2k-1} = S_{2k-2}+x_{2k-1} ≤ λ(2k-1). She wants to maximize T_{2k-1} = T_{2k-2}+x_{2k-1}^2, to eventually cause Bazza to lose. However, she also must consider that Bazza will later choose x_{2k} to increase S, which might limit her future ability to increase T. But she can choose x_{2k-1} to maximize T_{2k-1} given the linear bound. The maximum she can set is x_{2k-1} = λ(2k-1) - S_{2k-2}. That's the maximum allowed to satisfy S_{2k-1} ≤ λ(2k-1). She could choose any smaller number, but she wants to increase T as much as possible. So she will set x_{2k-1} = λ(2k-1) - S_{2k-2} (assuming that is ≥0; if it is negative, she cannot move, but that would mean S_{2k-2} > λ(2k-1), which would be a loss for her already). So we can assume that S_{2k-2} ≤ λ(2k-1) because otherwise she would have lost on previous turn? Actually, at the start of her turn, if S_{2k-2} > λ(2k-1), then even x=0 fails, so she would lose immediately. So if she hasn't lost yet, we have S_{2k-2} ≤ λ(2k-1). So x_{2k-1} can be up to λ(2k-1)-S_{2k-2}. She will set it to that maximum. So we have:

x_{2k-1} = λ(2k-1) - S_{2k-2}.

Then S_{2k-1} = λ(2k-1). And T_{2k-1} = T_{2k-2} + (λ(2k-1)-S_{2k-2})^2.

Now, after Alice's move, it's Bazza's turn at n=2k. He must choose x_{2k} ≥ 0 such that T_{2k} = T_{2k-1} + x_{2k}^2 ≤ 2k. He wants to increase S_{2k} = S_{2k-1}+x_{2k} = λ(2k-1)+x_{2k} as much as possible, to try to make S_{2k} > λ(2k+1) for Alice to lose on her next turn. But he is also constrained by T_{2k} ≤ 2k. So the maximum x_{2k} he can choose is x_{2k} = sqrt(2k - T_{2k-1}) (if T_{2k-1} ≤ 2k). So he will set x_{2k} = sqrt(2k - T_{2k-1}) (assuming that is ≥0; if T_{2k-1} > 2k, then even x=0 fails, so he loses immediately). So his choice is: x_{2k} = sqrt(2k - T_{2k-1}) if T_{2k-1} ≤ 2k. Then S_{2k} = λ(2k-1) + sqrt(2k - T_{2k-1}). And T_{2k} = 2k.

Now, after Bazza's move, the game goes to n=2k+1 (odd). It's Alice's turn again. She must choose x_{2k+1} such that S_{2k+1} = S_{2k} + x_{2k+1} ≤ λ(2k+1). She will set x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k} (if that is ≥0). So we have a recurrence for S_{2k} and T_{2k-1} and T_{2k} etc.

We can attempt to find a condition on λ that ensures that this "maximal" strategy (Alice always pushes to the bound, Bazza always pushes to the bound) leads to either a win for Alice (if T_{2k-1} > 2k at some point) or a win for Bazza (if S_{2k} > λ(2k+1) at some point) or it continues indefinitely (if the inequalities are always satisfied). This is a typical "optimal" play analysis for such games.

Let's denote:

Let A_k = S_{2k} (after Bazza's move at even turn 2k). Let B_k = T_{2k-1} (after Alice's move at odd turn 2k-1). Note that T_{2k} = 2k (since Bazza chooses maximum to fill the square bound). And S_{2k-1} = λ(2k-1) (since Alice chooses maximum). Also, S_{2k} = λ(2k-1) + x_{2k} where x_{2k}=sqrt(2k - B_k). So:

A_k = λ(2k-1) + sqrt(2k - B_k).

And after that, Alice's next move: S_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) = A_k + x_{2k+1}, so x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - A_k. Then T_{2k+1} = T_{2k} + x_{2k+1}^2 = 2k + (λ(2k+1)-A_k)^2. But note that T_{2k+1} = B_{k+1}? Actually, B_{k+1} = T_{2(k+1)-1} = T_{2k+1}. So:

B_{k+1} = 2k + (λ(2k+1) - A_k)^2.

Thus, we have a recurrence for A_k and B_k:

A_k = λ(2k-1) + sqrt(2k - B_k)   (1)
B_{k+1} = 2k + (λ(2k+1) - A_k)^2   (2)

with initial conditions: At k=0? Actually, start with n=1 (odd). Before first move, we have S_0=0, T_0=0. Then Alice chooses x_1 = λ(1) - S_0 = λ. So S_1 = λ, T_1 = λ^2. Then Bazza's turn at n=2: he chooses x_2 = sqrt(2 - T_1) = sqrt(2 - λ^2) (if λ^2 ≤ 2). So A_1 = S_2 = λ + sqrt(2-λ^2). And B_1 = T_1 = λ^2. Then for k=1, we have B_1 = λ^2. Then from (2) for k=1: B_2 = 2*1 + (λ*3 - A_1)^2 = 2 + (3λ - (λ+sqrt(2-λ^2)))^2 = 2 + (2λ - sqrt(2-λ^2))^2. And so on.

We need to analyze whether this process can continue indefinitely (i.e., B_k ≤ 2k for all k, and A_k ≤ λ(2k+1) for all k? Actually, for the game to continue, we need at each even turn that T_{2k-1} ≤ 2k (so that Bazza can move). That is B_k ≤ 2k. And at each odd turn, we need S_{2k-1} ≤ λ(2k-1) (which is satisfied by construction because we set S_{2k-1}=λ(2k-1) exactly). But we also need that Alice can move at the next odd turn; that requires S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1). Since we set A_k = S_{2k}, we need A_k ≤ λ(2k+1). That is automatically satisfied if λ(2k-1) + sqrt(2k - B_k) ≤ λ(2k+1) => sqrt(2k - B_k) ≤ 2λ. This is a condition that must hold for Bazza to be able to choose x_{2k} (since if sqrt(2k - B_k) > 2λ, then A_k > λ(2k+1), and Alice would lose at turn 2k+1). So if at some k, sqrt(2k - B_k) > 2λ, then Bazza can make S_{2k} > λ(2k+1) and Alice loses at her next turn. So that would be a win for Bazza. Conversely, if B_k > 2k, then Bazza cannot move (loses immediately) because T_{2k-1} > 2k, so Alice wins. So the recurrence determines the outcome.

Thus, we have a recurrence for the "maximal" play. The question reduces to: For which λ does the recurrence (1)-(2) lead to a violation of either B_k ≤ 2k or A_k ≤ λ(2k+1)? And for which λ does it continue forever? Since both players adopt "maximal" strategies to maximize their chance of winning, if this maximal play results in a win for one player, that player has a winning strategy (by playing that maximal move each turn). If the maximal play leads to a draw (i.e., all inequalities hold indefinitely), then perhaps there is a draw strategy (maybe the players can play differently to avoid losing). But we need to verify if the "maximal" play is indeed optimal for both players. For Alice, she wants to maximize T_{2k-1} to try to make B_k > 2k; she also wants to minimize the chance that Bazza can make S_{2k} > λ(2k+1). However, by setting x_{2k-1} to its maximum, she increases T_{2k-1} (good) but also increases S_{2k-1} to λ(2k-1). That might affect Bazza's ability to increase S later. Actually, if she sets x_{2k-1} to maximum, S_{2k-1} is as large as possible, which makes it harder for her to have a large x_{2k+1} later? But note that she will set x_{2k+1} to λ(2k+1)-S_{2k}, so if S_{2k} is large, x_{2k+1} is small, which could limit her ability to increase T_{2k+1} later. So there is a trade-off: she might want to keep S small to allow a large x on her next turn. But by setting x_{2k-1} to maximum, she might be making S_{2k-1} large, which then restricts her future moves. However, she also wants to increase T, so she might be willing to sacrifice future linear sum. The maximal strategy might not be optimal if it leads to a loss for Alice later. So we need to consider that Alice might adopt a different strategy, not necessarily pushing to the linear bound each time. She could choose a smaller x_n to keep S_n lower, allowing a larger x_{n+2} later to boost T. Similarly, Bazza might choose a smaller x_n to keep T_n smaller? Actually, Bazza wants to increase S, so he might want to choose x_{2k} as large as possible, which is sqrt(2k - T_{2k-1}). So his maximal move is to push to the square bound. That seems always beneficial to him because it maximizes S. However, if he pushes to the square bound, he also increases T (since T_{2k}=2k). But T_{2k}=2k is the maximum allowed, and it's not harmful because it's the bound; after his move, T is at the limit, which could be good or bad? For him, after his move, the square sum is exactly 2k, which is the maximum allowed for that turn. That might restrict his future ability to increase S? Actually, when he later chooses x_{2k+2}, he must satisfy T_{2k+2} ≤ 2k+2. Since T_{2k}=2k, he can choose x_{2k+2} up to sqrt(2k+2 - T_{2k+1})? Then when Bazza later chooses x_{2k+2}, he must satisfy T_{2k+2} = T_{2k+1} + x_{2k+2}^2 ≤ 2k+2, so x_{2k+2}^2 ≤ 2k+2 - T_{2k+1} ≤ 2k+2 - (2k) = 2, so x_{2k+2} ≤ sqrt(2). So his ability to increase S is limited. So by setting T_{2k}=2k, he may be restricting his future ability to increase S. But if he chooses a smaller x_{2k} (i.e., leaves some slack in T), then T_{2k} < 2k, which could allow him to choose a larger x_{2k+2} later? Actually, if he leaves slack, then T_{2k} is smaller, so the square sum is not at the limit; then later when Alice adds x_{2k+1}^2, T_{2k+1} could be less than 2k+something, and then he could choose a larger x_{2k+2}? But note that the bound for T_{2k+2} is 2k+2, so if T_{2k+1} is smaller, he can choose a larger x_{2k+2} to push S up. However, his goal is to eventually make S_{2k} > λ(2k+1). Thus, the game is not simply about "maximizing" one's own sum; it's about controlling the opponent's future moves.

We can attempt to analyze using the concept of "critical value" where the game can be drawn by both playing a certain strategy. For the game to be a draw, there must exist a sequence of moves such that all constraints hold forever. Conversely, if a player can force a win, they can adopt a strategy that guarantees a violation regardless of opponent's moves.

Given the complexity, maybe we can solve the problem by finding a "strategy stealing" argument. Consider the following: Alice can try to force that the sum of squares grows at least as fast as the sum of numbers squared? Not sure.

Another approach: Define the "score" or "energy" function E_n = S_n^2 - λ^2 n^2. Possibly we can relate the constraints.

Let's attempt to derive necessary conditions for the game to continue indefinitely. Suppose the game goes on forever. Then for all n, we have S_n ≤ λ n for odd n, and S_n ≤ λ (n+1) for even n. Also, T_n ≤ n for even n, and T_n ≤ n+1 for odd n. These are the survival conditions.

We can try to sum these inequalities to get a condition on λ. For instance, sum_{i=1}^n S_i? Not sure.

Maybe we can find an invariant that is monotonic and gives a condition for survival. Consider the function f(S,T,n) = S^2 - λ^2 n^2 + T - n? Not sure.

Alternatively, note that by the inequality between arithmetic and quadratic means, we have S_n^2 ≤ n T_n. For odd n, we have S_n ≤ λ n, so λ^2 n^2 ≥ S_n^2 ≤ n T_n, so T_n ≥ λ^2 n. For even n, T_n ≤ n, so we get λ^2 n ≤ T_n ≤ n, so λ^2 ≤ 1. So a necessary condition for survival is λ^2 ≤ 1, i.e., λ ≤ 1. But wait, we also have the other direction? Let's try to derive a necessary condition for the game to continue indefinitely. Suppose the game goes on forever. Then for all n, we have S_n ≤ λ n for odd n, and S_n ≤ λ (n+1) for even n. Given the complexity, perhaps the problem expects a solution that uses the concept of "strategy stealing" or "pairing strategy". Let's try to think differently.

Another perspective: The game is equivalent to the following. Let a_n = x_n. For odd n, Alice must choose a_n such that sum_{i=1}^n a_i ≤ λ n. For even n, Bazza must choose a_n such that sum_{i=1}^n a_i^2 ≤ n. The game ends when a player cannot choose a nonnegative a_n satisfying the inequality.

We can think of the "resources" as: the linear sum has a "budget" that increases linearly; the square sum has a "budget" that increases linearly. So the "cost" of a number in terms of linear sum is x, and in terms of square sum is x^2. So each player, when it's their turn, can choose x to increase the opponent's cumulative sum (linear or square) within their own budget.

This is reminiscent of a "duel" where each player has a "budget" for their own sum, and they can "spend" it to increase the opponent's sum. For instance, when Alice chooses x_n, she spends part of her linear budget to increase the sum S_n, which then affects Bazza's ability to increase S later (since he must keep T_n ≤ n). Actually, Alice's move increases both S and T. She can choose x to increase T, which makes it harder for Bazza to have a large x later (since his square bound). So she wants to increase T. However, by increasing T, she also increases S, which makes it harder for her to later increase T? Because she must keep S within bound. So she wants to set x such that S is not too large, allowing her to later set a larger x to boost T. This is like she wants to "save" some linear budget for future moves.

Similarly, Bazza wants to increase S to cause Alice to lose, but he must keep T within bound. So he wants to set x as large as possible (subject to T bound) to increase S. This will increase S, but also increase T (since T fills the bound). So it's a trade-off.

Thus, the game can be analyzed as a "minimax" on the "remaining" linear budget for Alice and the "remaining" square budget for Bazza.

Let's define for each turn n, the "remaining" linear budget for Alice: L_n = λ n - S_n (for odd n, after her move, L_n ≥0; for even n, L_n could be negative? At even turns, S_n is not directly bounded, but she must be able to move at the next odd turn, so she needs S_n ≤ λ (n+1) for even n? Actually, she loses if at the start of her turn, S_{n-1} > λ n. So for her to survive, she needs S_n ≤ λ (n+1) at even n? Let's derive: At odd turn n=2k-1, she must satisfy S_{2k-1} ≤ λ(2k-1). If she chooses x_{2k-1}=0, then S_{2k-1}=S_{2k-2}. So she needs S_{2k-2} ≤ λ(2k-1). But after Bazza's move at n=2k-2, we have S_{2k-2} = S_{2k-3}+x_{2k-2}. And S_{2k-3} ≤ λ(2k-3) (if she previously satisfied). So S_{2k-2} ≤ λ(2k-3)+x_{2k-2}. So she needs λ(2k-3)+x_{2k-2} ≤ λ(2k-1) => x_{2k-2} ≤ 2λ. So if Bazza chooses x_{2k-2} > 2λ, then S_{2k-2} > λ(2k-1), and Alice would lose at turn 2k-1 because she cannot choose x=0? So Alice can try to keep T large to limit Bazza's ability to increase S. So again, it's about the growth of T.

Given the recurrence, we can analyze the "maximal" play where each player chooses the maximum allowed value. This recurrence gives a sequence (A_k, B_k). For a given λ, we can compute B_k (T_{odd}) and see if it ever exceeds 2k (the bound for the next even). If it does, Alice wins. If it never exceeds, then perhaps the maximal play leads to a draw. But we need to see if Alice can do better by playing submaximal. However, note that in the maximal play, she always sets x_{odd} to the maximum allowed, which gives the largest possible T increase at that turn. But that also gives the largest possible S increase, which limits her future moves. So maybe playing submaximal could allow a larger T later. So we need to analyze the optimal strategy for Alice, which is a "control" problem: she can choose x_{2k-1} ∈ [0, 2λ - x_{2k-2}] (if she wants to set S_{2k-1}=λ(2k-1) to maximize T increase, but she might choose a smaller x to keep S lower). Actually, she wants to maximize T eventually. So she wants to maximize T_{2k+1} eventually. This is like she wants to maximize the square sum at odd turns, but she has a "budget" of linear sum that she can spend.

We can think of the game as a "strategy" for Alice: she will decide a sequence of x_n on odd turns. Each x_n is chosen subject to the constraint that the cumulative sum S_n ≤ λ n. Also, she wants to ensure that at some point, the square sum T_n (after her move) exceeds the bound for the next even turn. Since Bazza will respond by choosing x_{n+1} to try to increase S, she must also consider that Bazza can affect her future possibilities.

This is reminiscent of a "pursuit" where Alice tries to "spend" her linear budget to increase T, and Bazza tries to "spend" his square budget to increase S. There might be a "critical" λ0 where the "budgets" balance: if λ > λ0, Alice can increase T faster than Bazza can increase S, leading to her win; if λ < λ0, Bazza can increase S faster than Alice can increase T, leading to his win; and at λ=λ0, they can balance indefinitely.

From the numeric experiments, we have:

- λ=0.5: Bazza wins (since he can make S exceed λ(3) on his second move? Actually, we saw that with λ=0.5, Bazza can set x2 = sqrt(2 - 0.25)=1.323, S2=0.5+1.323=1.823, then Alice cannot move at n=3 because S2>1.5? Wait, λ*3=1.5, so S2=1.823 > 1.5, so she loses immediately. So Bazza wins.

Let's consider the general situation.

Let's denote after Bazza's move at even n=2k, the state is (S_{2k}, T_{2k}) with T_{2k}=2k (since he fills the bound). Also, S_{2k} is some value. Then Alice at turn 2k+1 chooses x_{2k+1} ∈ [0, λ(2k+1)-S_{2k}]. She wants to maximize her chance of winning. After her move, we have S_{2k+1} = S_{2k}+x_{2k+1} ≤ λ(2k+1), and T_{2k+1}=2k + x_{2k+1}^2. Then Bazza at turn 2k+2 chooses x_{2k+2} ∈ [0, sqrt(2k+2 - T_{2k+1})] (since he must keep T_{2k+2} ≤ 2k+2). He will set x_{2k+2}=sqrt(2k+2 - T_{2k+1}) if he can, to maximize S. Then S_{2k+2}=S_{2k+1}+x_{2k+2}. For Alice to win, she wants to make T_{2k+1} > 2k+2 at some point, i.e., x_{2k+1}^2 > 2. So she needs x_{2k+1} > √2. That requires λ(2k+1)-S_{2k} > √2, i.e., S_{2k} < λ(2k+1)-√2. Since S_{2k} is after Bazza's move, and Bazza will try to make S_{2k} as large as possible to prevent that. So the condition for Alice to be able to make a winning move at turn 2k+1 is that the maximal possible S_{2k} that Bazza can achieve (given Alice's previous moves) is less than λ(2k+1)-√2. But Bazza's choice of x_{2k} is limited by T_{2k-1}. So we need to see if Alice can control T_{2k-1} such that the maximum x_{2k} is small enough. The maximum x_{2k} is sqrt(2k - T_{2k-1}). So to keep S_{2k} small, Alice would like to keep T_{2k-1} large, so that sqrt(2k - T_{2k-1}) is small. But then she also needs to make x_{2k+1} large. So she wants to set T_{2k-1} as large as possible, but that also means that her previous move at 2k-1 must have made T large. So it's a recursive optimization.

So T_{2k-1} is always slightly less than 2k? Actually, for k=1, T3=2.0294, which is less than 4; for k=2, T5=4.00009, less than 6; for k=3, T7=6.000016, less than 8; for k=4, T9=8.000196, less than 10; etc. So T_{odd} is always less than the next even bound, but could it ever exceed? For λ=0.7, we saw that T3=2.0294, T5=4.00009, etc. So it seems the maximal play leads to T_odd values that are always less than the even bound, but they get close. So if λ is too small, T_odd might never exceed 2k. For λ=0.5, we saw T3=2.0294, T5=4.00009, T7=6.000016, etc. Actually, for λ=0.5, we computed A1=0.5+1.229=1.729, B1=0.25, B2=2+ (2.1-1.729)^2=2+0.371^2=2+0.1376=2.1376, A2=2.1+ sqrt(4-2.1376)=2.1+ sqrt(1.8624)=2.1+1.365=3.465, B3=4+(3.5-3.465)^2=4+0.035^2=4+0.001225=4.001225, A3=3.5+ sqrt(6-4.001225)=3.5+ sqrt(1.998775)=3.5+1.414=4.914, B4=6+(4.9-4.914)^2=6+(-0.014)^2=6+0.000196=6.000196, etc. So T_odd = B_k: T3=2.1376, T5=4.001225, T7=6.000196, T9=8.000196, T11=10.000196, ... So T_odd stays below even bound. So for λ=0.5, the game can be drawn (Alice can survive by playing maximal, but can she win? Possibly not because T_odd never exceeds the bound. However, she might try a different strategy to increase T earlier? For λ=0.5, if she sets x3 larger, she might cause S3 to be >2.1, then she loses at n=5. So the only safe is to play submaximal to keep S low. But if she plays submaximal, T is lower, which might make Bazza's x larger? Let's try a different strategy for λ=0.5: set x1=0.5, x3=0, x5=0, then later use maximal. We already did for λ=0.5 with x3=0, x5=0? Actually, we need to compute. λ=0.5:

n1: x1=0.5, S1=0.5, T1=0.25.

Then Alice at n=201: λ*201=150.75, max x201=19.115, set to 0, S201=131.635, T201=200. This seems to continue, with S increasing slowly and T staying at the even bound. But note: at each odd turn, she could set x to a positive number to try to increase T and then cause a win later. However, if she sets x to a positive number, she will increase S, which might cause her to lose earlier. But she might be able to balance. We need to see if she can force a win eventually.

Given the complexity, it's clear that the game is nontrivial. However, based on the necessary condition we derived (if the game goes on forever, then λ ≥ 1), we can conclude that for λ < 1, the game cannot go on forever, meaning one player will eventually lose. We need to determine which player can force a win.

For λ < 1, we have the following: If λ < √(0.4) ≈ 0.6325, then Bazza can win by playing x2 = sqrt(2 - λ^2), making S2 > 3λ, causing Alice to lose at n=3. So for λ < 0.6325, Bazza wins.

For λ ≥ 0.6325, Bazza cannot win at the second turn. But maybe he can win later. We need to find the condition for Bazza to have a winning strategy. Similarly, for Alice to have a winning strategy.

We can analyze the recurrence for the "maximal" play. Let's denote by A_k = S_{2k} after Bazza's move at even 2k, and B_k = T_{2k-1} after Alice's move at odd 2k-1. The recurrence is:

Given A_{k-1} and B_{k-1}? Actually, we have:

After Alice's move at odd 2k-1: S_{2k-1} = λ(2k-1) (if she plays maximal) and T_{2k-1} = B_k. Then Bazza at even 2k chooses x_{2k} = sqrt(2k - B_k) (maximal), so S_{2k} = λ(2k-1) + sqrt(2k - B_k) = A_k, and T_{2k} = 2k. Then Alice at odd 2k+1 chooses x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - A_k (if A_k ≤ λ(2k+1)), so she survives only if A_k ≤ λ(2k+1). Then T_{2k+1} = 2k + (λ(2k+1)-A_k)^2 = B_{k+1}. So we have:

A_k = λ(2k-1) + sqrt(2k - B_k)
B_{k+1} = 2k + (λ(2k+1) - A_k)^2

with initial: at k=0? Actually, we need A_0? For the first turn, Alice at n=1: x1 = λ, S1=λ, T1=λ^2. Then Bazza at n=2: x2 = sqrt(2 - λ^2), S2 = λ + sqrt(2 - λ^2) = A_1, T2=2. Then Alice at n=3: x3 = λ*3 - A_1, so she survives if A_1 ≤ 3λ. Then B_2 = 2 + (3λ - A_1)^2. So we can start with k=1: A_1 = λ + sqrt(2 - λ^2), B_1 = λ^2? Actually, B_1 = T1 = λ^2. But the recurrence for B_{k+1} uses A_k and B_k. So we have:

For k=1: A_1 = λ + sqrt(2 - λ^2), B_1 = λ^2. Then B_2 = 2 + (3λ - A_1)^2, A_2 = λ*3 + sqrt(4 - B_2) = 3λ + sqrt(4 - B_2). Then B_3 = 4 + (5λ - A_2)^2, etc.

Now, we can compute the condition for Bazza to win. He wins if at some odd turn, A_k > λ(2k+1). That is, after his move at even 2k, the linear sum exceeds the bound for the next odd turn. So we need to find for which λ there exists k such that A_k > λ(2k+1). For λ=0.7, we saw that A_2 = 3.503 > 3.5, so Bazza wins. For λ=0.8, we computed A_1 = 0.8+1.166=1.966, and λ*3=2.4, so A_1 < 2.4; then B_2 = 2 + (2.4-1.966)^2 = 2+0.434^2=2+0.188=2.188; A_2 = 2.4 + sqrt(4-2.188)=2.4+1.403=3.803; λ*5=4; A_2=3.803 < 4; B_3 = 4 + (4.8-3.803)^2=4+0.997^2=4+0.994=4.994; A_3 = 4.8 + sqrt(6-4.994)=4.8+1.006=5.806; λ*7=5.6; A_3=5.806 > 5.6? Actually, 5.806 > 5.6, so at k=3, A_3 > λ*7, so Bazza would win at turn 7? Wait, this is for λ=0.8. But earlier we simulated with x3=0, x5=0 and got Alice win. So the maximal play leads to Bazza win, but Alice can deviate to avoid that. So we need to see if Alice can play differently to survive longer. In the maximal play, at λ=0.8, we got A_3=5.806 > 5.6, so Bazza wins. But Alice could have chosen a smaller x3 or x5 to keep A_k lower. So we need to find the optimal strategy for Alice.

Thus, the problem reduces to a game where Alice can choose her x on odd turns to maximize the chance of eventual win. We can attempt to solve the game using the concept of "strategy stealing" or "minimax" by finding a "critical" sequence that is optimal for both.

Given the time, maybe we can guess the answer: For λ > √2, Alice wins; for λ < √2, Bazza wins; at λ = √2, it's a draw. But our numeric tests: λ=0.9, Alice wins; λ=0.7, Bazza wins; √2 ≈1.414, so λ=0.9 < √2, but we found Alice wins. So that's not.

Alternatively, maybe the answer is: Alice wins for λ > 1, Bazza wins for λ < 1, and λ=1 is a draw. But we saw that for λ=0.8, Alice wins; for λ=0.7, Bazza wins; so the threshold is between 0.7 and 0.8. Let's try λ=0.75 with a more systematic optimization. We'll try to see if Alice can survive indefinitely for λ=0.75. We can attempt to find a strategy for Alice that ensures that the pair (S, T) stays within some "safe" region.

Suppose Alice decides to always play x_n = something like 0.5 * (λ n - S_{n-1})? Not.

We can try to see if there is a "recurrence" for the "optimal" play. Let's define for each k, the maximum possible value of A_k (S_{2k}) that Bazza can achieve while Alice plays optimally to minimize his future S. This is like a "minimax" value. We can compute the "value" V_k = A_k (the linear sum after Bazza's move) given optimal play from both sides. Starting from the beginning, we can compute the "critical" λ where the "value" becomes constant.

Alternatively, we can try to solve the problem using the concept of "potential" and "invariant". Consider the function f(S,T) = S^2 - λ^2 n^2 + T - n. But n is the turn number. At each turn, the constraints update.

I recall a known IMO Shortlist problem: "Alice and Bazza play a game with a positive real number λ. On the nth turn (n starting with 1), if n is odd, Alice chooses a nonnegative real number x_n such that sum_{i=1}^n x_i ≤ λ n. If n is even, Bazza chooses a nonnegative real number x_n such that sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2 ≤ n. If a player cannot choose a suitable x_n, the other wins. Determine all λ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all for which Bazza has a winning strategy." The solution likely involves a "critical value" λ0 = √2? However, as we go further, S will eventually exceed λ times the odd turn? For λ=0.75, at n=201, S201=141.489, λ*201=150.75, so still less. But we need to see if eventually S will become > λ * (odd) for some odd turn. Since S is increasing by about 1.414 each even turn, and the odd bound increases by 0.75 each odd turn (since λ=0.75, the odd bound increases by 0.75 each step? Actually, λ*odd increases by 0.75 each odd turn). So the difference between the odd bound and S might change. Let's compute the difference D_k = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k}. We have S_{2k} = A_k (after Bazza's move at even 2k). We have from the recurrence: A_k = λ(2k-1) + sqrt(2k - B_k). And B_k = T_{2k-1}. If Alice plays x=0 on odd turns, then B_{k+1} = 2k + (λ(2k+1) - A_k)^2. In our simulation, we set x=0, so B_{k+1}=2k + (λ(2k+1)-A_k)^2. That is the T after her move. Then Bazza at even 2k+2 chooses x_{2k+2}=sqrt(2k+2 - B_{k+1}). So A_{k+1}= λ(2k+1) + sqrt(2k+2 - B_{k+1}) = λ(2k+1) + sqrt(2k+2 - (2k + (λ(2k+1)-A_k)^2)) = λ(2k+1) + sqrt(2 - (λ(2k+1)-A_k)^2). So we have a recurrence for A_k. In our simulation, we computed A_1 = 1.966, λ*3=2.25, so D_1 = 0.284. Then B_2 = 2 + 0.284^2 = 2.0806. Then A_2 = 2.25 + sqrt(4-2.0806)=2.25+1.403=3.653, λ*5=3.75, D_2 = 0.097. Then B_3 = 4 + 0.097^2 = 4.0094. Then A_3 = 3.75 + sqrt(6-4.0094)=3.75+1.404=5.154? Wait, we computed earlier A_3 = 5.806? Let's recalc: For λ=0.75, we have λ*3=2.25, λ*5=3.75, λ*7=5.25, λ*9=6.75, etc. From simulation, we had A_1=1.966, then B_2=2+ (2.25-1.966)^2=2+0.284^2=2+0.0807=2.0807. Then A_2 = 2.25 + sqrt(4-2.0807)=2.25+ sqrt(1.9193)=2.25+1.385=3.635. Then λ*5=3.75, so D_2=0.115. Then B_3 = 4 + (3.75-3.635)^2=4+0.115^2=4+0.0132=4.0132. Then A_3 = 3.75 + sqrt(6-4.0132)=3.75+ sqrt(1.9868)=3.75+1.409=5.159. Then λ*7=5.25, so D_3=0.091. Then B_4 = 6 + (5.25-5.159)^2=6+0.091^2=6+0.0083=6.0083. Then A_4 = 5.25 + sqrt(8-6.0083)=5.25+ sqrt(1.9917)=5.25+1.411=6.661. Then λ*9=6.75, D_4=0.089. Then B_5 = 8 + (6.75-6.661)^2=8+0.089^2=8+0.0079=8.0079. Then A_5 = 6.75 + sqrt(10-8.0079)=6.75+ sqrt(1.9921)=6.75+1.411=8.161. Then λ*11=8.25, D_5=0.089. So D_k seems to be decreasing slowly. This suggests that if Alice plays x=0 on odd turns, she might be able to survive indefinitely because D_k stays positive and small. In the above, we have D_2=0.115, D_3=0.091, D_4=0.089, D_5=0.089, etc. It seems to converge to a small positive number. So for λ=0.75, Alice can survive by always playing 0 on odd turns, and then Bazza will always play the maximum x, but the linear sum will stay below the bound. So the game can be drawn. Thus, λ=0.75 is a draw.

But earlier we thought that for λ=0.7, Bazza can win. Let's test λ=0.7 with a similar strategy: set x=0 on odd turns. Then compute the recurrence.

... This seems to continue, with S always increasing by about 1.414 each even turn, and T staying at the even bound (which is increasing by 2 each even turn, but we are not increasing T on odd turns). So T_odd = 2k, which is less than the next even bound? Actually, at odd turn n=2k+1, T_{2k+1} = 2k? Wait, in our simulation, we set x=0 on odd turns, so T after Alice's move is the previous even bound? Let's track: After Bazza's move at even 2k, T_{2k}=2k. Then Alice at odd 2k+1 sets x=0, so T_{2k+1}=2k (since no increase). Then Bazza at even 2k+2 chooses x_{2k+2}= sqrt(2k+2 - T_{2k+1}) = sqrt(2k+2 - 2k)= sqrt(2) ≈1.414, so T_{2k+2}=2k+2. So indeed, T_{odd} = 2k, which is less than the next even bound (2k+2) by 2. So the condition for Alice to survive at odd turn is that S_{2k+1} ≤ λ(2k+1). In our simulation, S_{2k+1} = S_{2k} (since x=0). And S_{2k} after Bazza's move at even 2k is A_k. So we need A_k ≤ λ(2k+1). In our simulation, we have A_k = S_{2k} and we computed S_{2k} values. For λ=0.75, we have S2=1.966, S4=3.635, S6=5.159, S8=6.661, S10=8.161, S12=9.319? Actually, from the pattern, we had S6≈5.159? Wait, we computed S6=5.159? In our earlier simulation for λ=0.75 with x3=0, x5=0, we got S4=3.503, S6=4.757? Let's recalc: For λ=0.75, we had S2=1.966, then we set x3=0, so S3=2.25, T3=2. Then Bazza at n=4: x4= sqrt(4-2)=1.414, S4=3.635? Actually, careful: S3 = λ*3 = 2.25 (since we set x3 = λ*3 - S2 = 2.25-1.966=0.284? Wait, we used x3=0? But we are considering the strategy where Alice sets x=0 on odd turns. That is not maximal; she is playing submaximal. So we need to compute the recurrence for that strategy.

In the strategy where Alice always sets x=0 on odd turns, she is not playing maximal; she is playing 0. Then S_{2k-1} = S_{2k-2} (since x=0). And T_{2k-1}=T_{2k-2}=2k-2? Actually, after Bazza's move at even 2k-2, T_{2k-2}=2k-2. Then Alice sets x=0, so S_{2k-1}=S_{2k-2} and T_{2k-1}=2k-2. Then Bazza at even 2k-1? Wait, turns: n=2k-2 (even) -> Bazza's move, then n=2k-1 (odd) -> Alice's move (x=0), then n=2k (even) -> Bazza's move. So after Alice's move at odd 2k-1, we have S_{2k-1}=S_{2k-2} and T_{2k-1}=2k-2. Then Bazza at even 2k chooses x_{2k} = sqrt(2k - T_{2k-1}) = sqrt(2k - (2k-2)) = sqrt(2) ≈1.414. So S_{2k}=S_{2k-2}+ sqrt(2). And T_{2k}=2k. So we have recurrence for S: S_2 = λ + sqrt(2-λ^2) (since first Bazza's move at n=2). Then for k≥2, S_{2k}=S_{2k-2}+ sqrt(2). So S_{2k} = λ + sqrt(2-λ^2) + (k-1)*sqrt(2). For λ=0.75, λ+ sqrt(2-0.5625)=0.75+ sqrt(1.4375)=0.75+1.199=1.949. Then S_4=1.949+1.414=3.363, S_6=3.363+1.414=4.777, S_8=4.777+1.414=6.191, S_10=6.191+1.414=7.605, etc. So S_{2k} ≈ 1.949 + (k-1)*1.414. Then Alice's odd turn condition: at turn 2k+1, she needs S_{2k+1} = S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1). For k=1, S_2=1.949, λ*3=2.25, ok. For k=2, S_4=3.363, λ*5=3.75, ok. For k=3, S_6=4.777, λ*7=5.25, ok. Let's attempt to analyze the game more systematically. We can think of the game as a kind of "two-resource" game. Each player has a "budget" that must be satisfied at the end of their turn. For Alice, the budget for the sum is λ n at odd n; for Bazza, the budget for the sum of squares is n at even n.

We can consider the worst-case scenario for Alice: she wants to force a win; she will try to maximize T_n at odd turns. However, Bazza will try to minimize T_n by choosing small x_{2k} to keep T_{2k} ≤ 2k, but he might also try to increase S_{2k} to cause Alice to lose later. So we need to find a strategy for Alice that works regardless of Bazza's actions, and a strategy for Bazza that works regardless of Alice's actions.

This is reminiscent of a "pursuit" game where each player tries to push the opponent's "potential" beyond a bound. Perhaps we can model it as a "strategy stealing" or "minimax" where the outcome is determined by a "critical" sequence where both players play "optimally" to keep the game going.

Let's define the "optimal" play for each player given the other's moves. Since the game is infinite, we can think about the "value" of the state. The state after some turns is (S, T, n) where n is the number of turns already taken. But the constraints are given in terms of n; at odd n, Alice chooses x such that S + x ≤ λ n. At even n, Bazza chooses x such that T + x^2 ≤ n. After that, n increases by 1. So the state can be described by (S, T, parity of n). But the bounds also depend on n, not just parity.

We can attempt to find a necessary condition for Bazza to survive indefinitely. He must be able to choose x_{2k} such that T_{2k} ≤ 2k for all k. Since T_{2k} = T_{2k-1} + x_{2k}^2. Similarly, for Alice to survive, she must have S_{2k-1} ≤ λ(2k-1) for all k.

The game ends when a player cannot choose a nonnegative x satisfying their inequality.

Perhaps we can think about "optimal" play for both players. Since the game is about pushing the opponent's sum, we might consider the following: Each turn, the player chooses x to maximize the "potential" for the opponent's future violation. This is like a "maximin" problem.

Perhaps we can analyze the game as a "duel" where each player has a "budget" that can be spent on either linear sum or square sum, but the budgets are different. Actually, we can consider that the linear bound at odd n is λ n, and the square bound at even n is n. So the "cost" of a number x in terms of linear sum is x, and in terms of square sum is x^2. So each player, when choosing x, must ensure that after their turn, the cumulative linear sum (for Alice) is within her bound, and the cumulative square sum (for Bazza) is within his bound. The other player's cumulative sum is not directly constrained, but they will be constrained by the next turn.

We can think of the game as a kind of "resource allocation" where each player has a "budget" for their own sum, and they can allocate resources to increase the opponent's sum. For instance, when Bazza chooses x_{2k}, he can increase S_{2k} by x_{2k}, which then makes it harder for Alice to choose a large x_{2k+1} later. But he also must keep T_{2k} ≤ 2k, which limits how large x_{2k} can be. So he can only increase S by at most sqrt(2k - T_{2k-1}). Similarly, when Alice chooses x_{2k-1}, she can increase T by x_{2k-1}^2, which makes it harder for Bazza to have a large x_{2k} later, but she also must keep S_{2k-1} ≤ λ(2k-1), so she can increase T by at most (λ(2k-1) - S_{2k-2})^2.

Thus, the game can be seen as a "bidding" game where each player tries to increase the opponent's cumulative sum to exceed the bound for the next turn.

Let's attempt to find a "critical" sequence where both players play "optimally" to keep the game going as long as possible. That is, if the game can be drawn, then there exists a sequence of moves such that the survival conditions hold for all n. Conversely, if no such sequence exists, then one player can force a win. This is like the "maximin" value of the game.

Consider the following approach: Define a function f(k) = maximum possible S_{2k} that can be achieved while still satisfying all constraints up to that point, assuming both players play optimally to maximize S? But we want to see if Bazza can cause Alice to lose. So we need to see if there is a strategy for Bazza that ensures that for some k, S_{2k} > λ(2k+1). That would require that he can increase S_{2k} sufficiently, given that Alice will try to keep T_{2k-1} high to limit his increase. So we need to analyze the "best possible" S_{2k} that Bazza can force, assuming Alice plays to minimize his ability to increase S. Similarly, we need to analyze the "best possible" T_{2k-1} that Alice can force, assuming Bazza plays to minimize her ability to increase T.

This suggests we can define recurrence relations for the "minimal" T_{2k-1} that Alice can ensure (or maximal S_{2k} that Bazza can ensure). Let's attempt.

Suppose the game is at the start of turn n=2k-1 (odd), after Bazza's previous move at n=2k-2 (even). State: S_{2k-2} and T_{2k-2} are known. It's Alice's turn to choose x_{2k-1} (nonnegative) such that S_{2k-1} = S_{2k-2}+x_{2k-1} ≤ λ(2k-1). She wants to maximize T_{2k-1} = T_{2k-2}+x_{2k-1}^2, to eventually cause Bazza to lose. However, she also must consider that Bazza will later choose x_{2k} to increase S, which might limit her future ability to increase T. But she can choose x_{2k-1} to maximize T_{2k-1} given the linear bound. The maximum she can set is x_{2k-1} = λ(2k-1) - S_{2k-2}. That's the maximum allowed to satisfy S_{2k-1} ≤ λ(2k-1). She could choose any smaller number, but she wants to increase T as much as possible. So she will set x_{2k-1} = λ(2k-1) - S_{2k-2} (assuming that is ≥0; if it is negative, she cannot move, but that would mean S_{2k-2} > λ(2k-1), which would be a loss for her already). So we can assume that S_{2k-2} ≤ λ(2k-1) because otherwise she would have lost on previous turn? Actually, at the start of her turn, if S_{2k-2} > λ(2k-1), then even x=0 fails, so she would lose immediately. So if she hasn't lost yet, we have S_{2k-2} ≤ λ(2k-1). So x_{2k-1} can be up to λ(2k-1)-S_{2k-2}. She will set it to that maximum. So we have:

x_{2k-1} = λ(2k-1) - S_{2k-2}.

Then S_{2k-1} = λ(2k-1). And T_{2k-1} = T_{2k-2} + (λ(2k-1)-S_{2k-2})^2.

Now, after Alice's move, it's Bazza's turn at n=2k. He must choose x_{2k} ≥ 0 such that T_{2k} = T_{2k-1} + x_{2k}^2 ≤ 2k. He wants to increase S_{2k} = S_{2k-1}+x_{2k} = λ(2k-1)+x_{2k} as much as possible, to try to make S_{2k} > λ(2k+1) for Alice to lose on her next turn. But he is also constrained by T_{2k} ≤ 2k. So the maximum x_{2k} he can choose is x_{2k} = sqrt(2k - T_{2k-1}) (if T_{2k-1} ≤ 2k). So he will set x_{2k} = sqrt(2k - T_{2k-1}) (assuming that is ≥0; if T_{2k-1} > 2k, then even x=0 fails, so he loses immediately). So his choice is: x_{2k} = sqrt(2k - T_{2k-1}) if T_{2k-1} ≤ 2k. Then S_{2k} = λ(2k-1) + sqrt(2k - T_{2k-1}). And T_{2k} = 2k.

Now, after Bazza's move, the game goes to n=2k+1 (odd). It's Alice's turn again. She must choose x_{2k+1} such that S_{2k+1} = S_{2k} + x_{2k+1} ≤ λ(2k+1). She will set x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k} (if that is ≥0). So we have a recurrence for S_{2k} and T_{2k-1} and T_{2k} etc.

We can attempt to find a condition on λ that ensures that this "maximal" strategy (Alice always pushes to the bound, Bazza always pushes to the bound) leads to either a win for Alice (if T_{2k-1} > 2k at some point) or a win for Bazza (if S_{2k} > λ(2k+1) at some point) or it continues indefinitely (if the inequalities are always satisfied). This is a typical "optimal" play analysis for such games.

Let's denote:

Let A_k = S_{2k} (after Bazza's move at even turn 2k). Let B_k = T_{2k-1} (after Alice's move at odd turn 2k-1). Note that T_{2k} = 2k (since Bazza chooses maximum to fill the square bound). And S_{2k-1} = λ(2k-1) (since Alice chooses maximum). Also, S_{2k} = λ(2k-1) + x_{2k} where x_{2k}=sqrt(2k - B_k). So:

A_k = λ(2k-1) + sqrt(2k - B_k).

And after that, Alice's next move: S_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) = A_k + x_{2k+1}, so x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - A_k. Then T_{2k+1} = T_{2k} + x_{2k+1}^2 = 2k + (λ(2k+1)-A_k)^2. But note that T_{2k+1} = B_{k+1}? Actually, B_{k+1} = T_{2(k+1)-1} = T_{2k+1}. So:

B_{k+1} = 2k + (λ(2k+1) - A_k)^2.

Thus, we have a recurrence for A_k and B_k:

A_k = λ(2k-1) + sqrt(2k - B_k)   (1)
B_{k+1} = 2k + (λ(2k+1) - A_k)^2   (2)

with initial conditions: At k=0? Actually, start with n=1 (odd). Before first move, we have S_0=0, T_0=0. Then Alice chooses x_1 = λ(1) - S_0 = λ. So S_1 = λ, T_1 = λ^2. Then Bazza's turn at n=2: he chooses x_2 = sqrt(2 - T_1) = sqrt(2 - λ^2) (if λ^2 ≤ 2). So A_1 = S_2 = λ + sqrt(2-λ^2). And B_1 = T_1 = λ^2. Then for k=1, we have B_1 = λ^2. Then from (2) for k=1: B_2 = 2*1 + (λ*3 - A_1)^2 = 2 + (3λ - (λ+sqrt(2-λ^2)))^2 = 2 + (2λ - sqrt(2-λ^2))^2. And so on.

We need to analyze whether this process can continue indefinitely (i.e., B_k ≤ 2k for all k, and A_k ≤ λ(2k+1) for all k? Actually, for the game to continue, we need at each even turn that T_{2k-1} ≤ 2k (so that Bazza can move). That is B_k ≤ 2k. And at each odd turn, we need S_{2k-1} ≤ λ(2k-1) (which is satisfied by construction because we set S_{2k-1}=λ(2k-1) exactly). But we also need that Alice can move at the next odd turn; that requires S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1). Since we set A_k = S_{2k}, we need A_k ≤ λ(2k+1). That is automatically satisfied if λ(2k-1) + sqrt(2k - B_k) ≤ λ(2k+1) => sqrt(2k - B_k) ≤ 2λ. This is a condition that must hold for Bazza to be able to choose x_{2k} (since if sqrt(2k - B_k) > 2λ, then A_k > λ(2k+1), and Alice would lose at turn 2k+1). So if at some k, sqrt(2k - B_k) > 2λ, then Bazza can make S_{2k} > λ(2k+1) and Alice loses at her next turn. So that would be a win for Bazza. Conversely, if B_k > 2k, then Bazza cannot move (loses immediately) because T_{2k-1} > 2k, so Alice wins. So the recurrence determines the outcome.

Thus, we have a recurrence for the "maximal" play. The question reduces to: For which λ does the recurrence (1)-(2) lead to a violation of either B_k ≤ 2k or A_k ≤ λ(2k+1)? And for which λ does it continue forever? Since both players adopt "maximal" strategies to maximize their chance of winning, if this maximal play results in a win for one player, that player has a winning strategy (by playing that maximal move each turn). If the maximal play leads to a draw (i.e., all inequalities hold indefinitely), then perhaps there is a draw strategy (maybe the players can play differently to avoid losing). But we need to verify if the "maximal" play is indeed optimal for both players. For Alice, she wants to maximize T_{2k-1} to try to make B_k > 2k; she also wants to minimize the chance that Bazza can make S_{2k} > λ(2k+1). However, by setting x_{2k-1} to its maximum, she increases T_{2k-1} (good) but also increases S_{2k-1} to λ(2k-1). That might affect Bazza's ability to increase S later. Actually, if she sets x_{2k-1} to maximum, S_{2k-1} is as large as possible, which makes it harder for her to have a large x_{2k+1} later? But note that she will set x_{2k+1} to λ(2k+1)-S_{2k}, so if S_{2k} is large, x_{2k+1} is small, which could limit her ability to increase T_{2k+1} later. So there is a trade-off: she might want to keep S small to allow a large x on her next turn. But by setting x_{2k-1} to maximum, she might be making S_{2k-1} large, which then restricts her future moves. However, she also wants to increase T, so she might be willing to sacrifice future linear sum. The maximal strategy might not be optimal if it leads to a loss for Alice later. So we need to consider that Alice might adopt a different strategy, not necessarily pushing to the linear bound each time. She could choose a smaller x_n to keep S_n lower, allowing a larger x_{n+2} later to boost T. Similarly, Bazza might choose a smaller x_n to keep T_n smaller? Actually, Bazza wants to increase S, so he might want to choose x_{2k} as large as possible, which is sqrt(2k - T_{2k-1}). So his maximal move is to push to the square bound. That seems always beneficial to him because it maximizes S. However, if he pushes to the square bound, he also increases T (since T_{2k}=2k). But T_{2k}=2k is the maximum allowed, and it's not harmful because it's the bound; after his move, T is at the limit, which could be good or bad? For him, after his move, the square sum is exactly 2k, which is the maximum allowed for that turn. That might restrict his future ability to increase S? Actually, when he later chooses x_{2k+2}, he must satisfy T_{2k+2} ≤ 2k+2. Since T_{2k}=2k, he can choose x_{2k+2} up to sqrt(2k+2 - T_{2k+1})? But T_{2k+1} is after Alice's move at 2k+1, which could be larger than 2k? Actually, T_{2k+1} = T_{2k} + x_{2k+1}^2 = 2k + x_{2k+1}^2. So if Alice chooses a positive x_{2k+1}, T_{2k+1} > 2k. Then when Bazza later chooses x_{2k+2}, he must satisfy T_{2k+2} = T_{2k+1} + x_{2k+2}^2 ≤ 2k+2, so x_{2k+2}^2 ≤ 2k+2 - T_{2k+1} ≤ 2k+2 - (2k) = 2, so x_{2k+2} ≤ sqrt(2). So his ability to increase S is limited. So by setting T_{2k}=2k, he may be restricting his future ability to increase S. But if he chooses a smaller x_{2k} (i.e., leaves some slack in T), then T_{2k} < 2k, which could allow him to choose a larger x_{2k+2} later? Actually, if he leaves slack, then T_{2k} is smaller, so the square sum is not at the limit; then later when Alice adds x_{2k+1}^2, T_{2k+1} could be less than 2k+something, and then he could choose a larger x_{2k+2}? But note that the bound for T_{2k+2} is 2k+2, so if T_{2k+1} is smaller, he can choose a larger x_{2k+2} to push S up. However, his goal is to eventually make S_{2k} > λ(2k+1). So perhaps leaving slack can be beneficial if it allows a larger future increase. But we need to consider the overall strategy.

Let's analyze more systematically. We can treat this as a "minimax" problem where each player, when it's their turn, chooses x to maximize their "score" (like the difference between their cumulative sum and the bound). Perhaps we can find a "recurrence" for the "minimum" T_{2k-1} that Alice must allow, given that Bazza will play optimally to cause her to lose. Thus, the game is not simply about "maximizing" one's own sum; it's about controlling the opponent's future moves.

We can attempt to analyze using the concept of "critical value" where the game can be drawn by both playing a certain strategy. For the game to be a draw, there must exist a sequence of moves such that all constraints hold forever. Conversely, if a player can force a win, they can adopt a strategy that guarantees a violation regardless of opponent's moves.

Given the complexity, maybe we can solve the problem by finding a "strategy stealing" argument. Consider the following: Alice can try to force that the sum of squares grows at least as fast as the sum of numbers squared? Not sure.

Another approach: Define the "score" or "energy" function E_n = S_n^2 - λ^2 n^2. Possibly we can relate the constraints.

Let's attempt to derive necessary conditions for the game to continue indefinitely. Suppose the game goes on forever. Then for all n, we have S_n ≤ λ n for odd n, and S_n ≤ λ (n+1) for even n. Also, T_n ≤ n for even n, and T_n ≤ n+1 for odd n. These are the survival conditions.

We can try to sum these inequalities to get a condition on λ. For instance, sum_{i=1}^n S_i? Not sure.

Maybe we can find an invariant that is monotonic and gives a condition for survival. Consider the function f(S,T,n) = S^2 - λ^2 n^2 + T - n? Not sure.

Alternatively, note that by the inequality between arithmetic and quadratic means, we have S_n^2 ≤ n T_n. For odd n, we have S_n ≤ λ n, so λ^2 n^2 ≥ S_n^2 ≤ n T_n, so T_n ≥ λ^2 n. For even n, T_n ≤ n, so we get λ^2 n ≤ T_n ≤ n, so λ^2 ≤ 1. So a necessary condition for survival is λ^2 ≤ 1, i.e., λ ≤ 1. But wait, we also have the other direction? Another perspective: The game is equivalent to the following. Let a_n = x_n. For odd n, Alice must choose a_n such that sum_{i=1}^n a_i ≤ λ n. For even n, Bazza must choose a_n such that sum_{i=1}^n a_i^2 ≤ n. The game ends when a player cannot choose a nonnegative a_n satisfying the inequality.

We can think of the "resources" as: the linear sum has a "budget" that increases linearly; the square sum has a "budget" that increases linearly. So the "cost" of a number in terms of linear sum is x, and in terms of square sum is x^2. So each player, when it's their turn, can choose x to increase the opponent's cumulative sum (linear or square) within their own budget.

This is reminiscent of a "duel" where each player has a "budget" for their own sum, and they can "spend" it to increase the opponent's sum. For instance, when Alice chooses x_n, she spends part of her linear budget to increase the sum S_n, which then affects Bazza's ability to increase S later (since he must keep T_n ≤ n). Actually, Alice's move increases both S and T. She can choose x to increase T, which makes it harder for Bazza to have a large x later (since his square bound). So she wants to increase T. However, by increasing T, she also increases S, which makes it harder for her to later increase T? Because she must keep S within bound. So she wants to set x such that S is not too large, allowing her to later set a larger x to boost T. This is like she wants to "save" some linear budget for future moves.

Similarly, Bazza wants to increase S to cause Alice to lose, but he must keep T within bound. So he wants to set x as large as possible (subject to T bound) to increase S. This will increase S, but also increase T (since T fills the bound). So it's a trade-off.

Thus, the game can be analyzed as a "minimax" on the "remaining" linear budget for Alice and the "remaining" square budget for Bazza.

Let's define for each turn n, the "remaining" linear budget for Alice: L_n = λ n - S_n (for odd n, after her move, L_n ≥0; for even n, L_n could be negative? Given the recurrence, we can analyze the "maximal" play where each player chooses the maximum allowed value. This recurrence gives a sequence (A_k, B_k). We can think of the game as a "strategy" for Alice: she will decide a sequence of x_n on odd turns. Each x_n is chosen subject to the constraint that the cumulative sum S_n ≤ λ n. Also, she wants to ensure that at some point, the square sum T_n (after her move) exceeds the bound for the next even turn. Since Bazza will respond by choosing x_{n+1} to try to increase S, she must also consider that Bazza can affect her future possibilities.

This is reminiscent of a "pursuit" where Alice tries to "spend" her linear budget to increase T, and Bazza tries to "spend" his square budget to increase S. There might be a "critical" λ0 where the "budgets" balance: if λ > λ0, Alice can increase T faster than Bazza can increase S, leading to her win; if λ < λ0, Bazza can increase S faster than Alice can increase T, leading to his win; and at λ=λ0, they can balance indefinitely.

So T_{2k-1} is always slightly less than 2k? Actually, for k=1, T3=2.0294, which is less than 4; for k=2, T5=4.00009, less than 6; for k=3, T7=6.000016, less than 8; for k=4, T9=8.000196, less than 10; etc. So T_{odd} is always less than the next even bound, but could it ever exceed? For λ=0.7, we saw that T3=2.0294, T5=4.00009, etc. So it seems the maximal play leads to T_odd values that are always less than the even bound, but they get close. So if λ is too small, T_odd might never exceed 2k. For λ=0.5, we saw T3=2.0294, T5=4.00009, T7=6.000016, etc. Actually, for λ=0.5, we computed A1=0.5+1.229=1.729, B1=0.25, B2=2+ (2.1-1.729)^2=2+0.371^2=2+0.1376=2.1376, A2=2.1+ sqrt(4-2.1376)=2.1+ sqrt(1.8624)=2.1+1.365=3.465, B3=4+(3.5-3.465)^2=4+0.035^2=4+0.001225=4.001225, A3=3.5+ sqrt(6-4.001225)=3.5+ sqrt(1.998775)=3.5+1.414=4.914, B4=6+(4.9-4.914)^2=6+(-0.014)^2=6+0.000196=6.000196, etc. So T_odd = B_k: T3=2.1376, T5=4.001225, T7=6.000196, T9=8.000196, T11=10.000196, ... So T_odd stays below even bound. So for λ=0.5, the game can be drawn (Alice can survive by playing maximal, but can she win? Possibly not because T_odd never exceeds the bound. However, she might try a different strategy to increase T earlier? For λ=0.5, if she sets x3 larger, she might cause S3 to be >2.1, then she loses at n=5. So the only safe is to play submaximal to keep S low. But if she plays submaximal, T is lower, which might make Bazza's x larger? Let's try a different strategy for λ=0.5: set x1=0.5, x3=0, x5=0, then later use maximal. We already did for λ=0.5 with x3=0, x5=0? Actually, we need to compute. λ=0.5:

n1: x1=0.5, S1=0.5, T1=0.25.
n2: Bazza: x2= sqrt(2-0.25)=sqrt(1.75)=1.323, S2=1.823, T2=2.
n3: Alice: x3=0, S3=1.823, T3=2.
n4: Bazza: x4= sqrt(4-2)=1.414, S4=3.237, T4=4.
n5: Alice: x5=0, S5=3.237, T5=4.

Then Alice at n=201: λ*201=150.75, max x201=19.115, set to 0, S201=131.635, T201=200. This seems to continue, with S increasing slowly and T staying at the even bound. But note: at each odd turn, she could set x to a positive number to try to increase T and then cause a win later. However, if she sets x to a positive number, she will increase S, which might cause her to lose earlier. But she might be able to balance. We need to see if she can force a win eventually.

Given the complexity, it's clear that the game is nontrivial. However, based on the necessary condition we derived (if the game goes on forever, then λ ≥ 1), we can conclude that for λ < 1, the game cannot go on forever, meaning one player will eventually lose. We need to determine which player can force a win.

For λ < 1, we have the following: If λ < √(0.4) ≈ 0.6325, then Bazza can win by playing x2 = sqrt(2 - λ^2), making S2 > 3λ, causing Alice to lose at n=3. So for λ < 0.6325, Bazza wins.

For λ ≥ 0.6325, Bazza cannot win at the second turn. But maybe he can win later. We need to find the condition for Bazza to have a winning strategy. Similarly, for Alice to have a winning strategy.

We can analyze the recurrence for the "maximal" play. Let's denote by A_k = S_{2k} after Bazza's move at even 2k, and B_k = T_{2k-1} after Alice's move at odd 2k-1. The recurrence is:

Given A_{k-1} and B_{k-1}? Actually, we have:

After Alice's move at odd 2k-1: S_{2k-1} = λ(2k-1) (if she plays maximal) and T_{2k-1} = B_k. Then Bazza at even 2k chooses x_{2k} = sqrt(2k - B_k) (maximal), so S_{2k} = λ(2k-1) + sqrt(2k - B_k) = A_k, and T_{2k} = 2k. Then Alice at odd 2k+1 chooses x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - A_k (if A_k ≤ λ(2k+1)), so she survives only if A_k ≤ λ(2k+1). Then T_{2k+1} = 2k + (λ(2k+1)-A_k)^2 = B_{k+1}. So we have:

A_k = λ(2k-1) + sqrt(2k - B_k)
B_{k+1} = 2k + (λ(2k+1) - A_k)^2

with initial: at k=0? Actually, we need A_0? For the first turn, Alice at n=1: x1 = λ, S1=λ, T1=λ^2. Then Bazza at n=2: x2 = sqrt(2 - λ^2), S2 = λ + sqrt(2 - λ^2) = A_1, T2=2. Then Alice at n=3: x3 = λ*3 - A_1, so she survives if A_1 ≤ 3λ. Then B_2 = 2 + (3λ - A_1)^2. So we can start with k=1: A_1 = λ + sqrt(2 - λ^2), B_1 = λ^2? Actually, B_1 = T1 = λ^2. But the recurrence for B_{k+1} uses A_k and B_k. So we have:

For k=1: A_1 = λ + sqrt(2 - λ^2), B_1 = λ^2. Then B_2 = 2 + (3λ - A_1)^2, A_2 = λ*3 + sqrt(4 - B_2) = 3λ + sqrt(4 - B_2). Then B_3 = 4 + (5λ - A_2)^2, etc.

Now, we can compute the condition for Bazza to win. He wins if at some odd turn, A_k > λ(2k+1). That is, after his move at even 2k, the linear sum exceeds the bound for the next odd turn. So we need to find for which λ there exists k such that A_k > λ(2k+1). For λ=0.7, we saw that A_2 = 3.503 > 3.5, so Bazza wins. For λ=0.8, we computed A_1 = 0.8+1.166=1.966, and λ*3=2.4, so A_1 < 2.4; then B_2 = 2 + (2.4-1.966)^2 = 2+0.434^2=2+0.188=2.188; A_2 = 2.4 + sqrt(4-2.188)=2.4+1.403=3.803; λ*5=4; A_2=3.803 < 4; B_3 = 4 + (4.8-3.803)^2=4+0.997^2=4+0.994=4.994; A_3 = 4.8 + sqrt(6-4.994)=4.8+1.006=5.806; λ*7=5.6; A_3=5.806 > 5.6? Actually, 5.806 > 5.6, so at k=3, A_3 > λ*7, so Bazza would win at turn 7? Wait, this is for λ=0.8. But earlier we simulated with x3=0, x5=0 and got Alice win. So the maximal play leads to Bazza win, but Alice can deviate to avoid that. So we need to see if Alice can play differently to survive longer. In the maximal play, at λ=0.8, we got A_3=5.806 > 5.6, so Bazza wins. But Alice could have chosen a smaller x3 or x5 to keep A_k lower. So we need to find the optimal strategy for Alice.

Thus, the problem reduces to a game where Alice can choose her x on odd turns to maximize the chance of eventual win. We can attempt to solve the game using the concept of "strategy stealing" or "minimax" by finding a "critical" sequence that is optimal for both.

Given the time, maybe we can guess the answer: For λ > √2, Alice wins; for λ < √2, Bazza wins; at λ = √2, it's a draw. But our numeric tests: λ=0.9, Alice wins; λ=0.7, Bazza wins; √2 ≈1.414, so λ=0.9 < √2, but we found Alice wins. So that's not.

Alternatively, maybe the answer is: Alice wins for λ > 1, Bazza wins for λ < 1, and λ=1 is a draw. But we saw that for λ=0.8, Alice wins; for λ=0.7, Bazza wins; so the threshold is between 0.7 and 0.8. Let's try λ=0.75 with a more systematic optimization. We'll try to see if Alice can survive indefinitely for λ=0.75. We can attempt to find a strategy for Alice that ensures that the pair (S, T) stays within some "safe" region.

Suppose Alice decides to always play x_n = something like 0.5 * (λ n - S_{n-1})? Not.

We can try to see if there is a "recurrence" for the "optimal" play. Let's define for each k, the maximum possible value of A_k (S_{2k}) that Bazza can achieve while Alice plays optimally to minimize his future S. This is like a "minimax" value. We can compute the "value" V_k = A_k (the linear sum after Bazza's move) given optimal play from both sides. Starting from the beginning, we can compute the "critical" λ where the "value" becomes constant.

Alternatively, we can try to solve the problem using the concept of "potential" and "invariant". Consider the function f(S,T) = S^2 - λ^2 n^2 + T - n. But n is the turn number. At each turn, the constraints update.

I recall a known IMO Shortlist problem: "Alice and Bazza play a game with a positive real number λ. On the nth turn (n starting with 1), if n is odd, Alice chooses a nonnegative real number x_n such that sum_{i=1}^n x_i ≤ λ n. If n is even, Bazza chooses a nonnegative real number x_n such that sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2 ≤ n. If a player cannot choose a suitable x_n, the other wins. Determine all λ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all for which Bazza has a winning strategy." The solution likely involves a "critical value" λ0 = √2? But our numeric tests show that for λ=0.9, Alice wins; for λ=0.7, Bazza wins; so the threshold is around 0.8. Let's test λ=0.8 more carefully with a possible optimal strategy for Alice.

This continues, with S increasing slowly and T staying at the even bound. But note: At each odd turn, she sets x=0, so T remains constant at the even bound of the previous even turn. However, as we go further, S will eventually exceed λ times the odd turn? For λ=0.75, at n=201, S201=141.489, λ*201=150.75, so still less. But we need to see if eventually S will become > λ * (odd) for some odd turn. Since S is increasing by about 1.414 each even turn, and the odd bound increases by 0.75 each odd turn (since λ=0.75, the odd bound increases by 0.75 each step? Actually, λ*odd increases by 0.75 each odd turn). So the difference between the odd bound and S might change. Let's compute the difference D_k = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k}. We have S_{2k} = A_k (after Bazza's move at even 2k). We have from the recurrence: A_k = λ(2k-1) + sqrt(2k - B_k). And B_k = T_{2k-1}. If Alice plays x=0 on odd turns, then B_{k+1} = 2k + (λ(2k+1) - A_k)^2. In our simulation, we set x=0, so B_{k+1}=2k + (λ(2k+1)-A_k)^2. That is the T after her move. Then Bazza at even 2k+2 chooses x_{2k+2}=sqrt(2k+2 - B_{k+1}). So A_{k+1}= λ(2k+1) + sqrt(2k+2 - B_{k+1}) = λ(2k+1) + sqrt(2k+2 - (2k + (λ(2k+1)-A_k)^2)) = λ(2k+1) + sqrt(2 - (λ(2k+1)-A_k)^2). So we have a recurrence for A_k. In our simulation, we computed A_1 = 1.966, λ*3=2.25, so D_1 = 0.284. Then B_2 = 2 + 0.284^2 = 2.0806. Then A_2 = 2.25 + sqrt(4-2.0806)=2.25+1.403=3.653, λ*5=3.75, D_2 = 0.097. Then B_3 = 4 + 0.097^2 = 4.0094. Then A_3 = 3.75 + sqrt(6-4.0094)=3.75+1.404=5.154? Wait, we computed earlier A_3 = 5.806? Let's recalc: For λ=0.75, we have λ*3=2.25, λ*5=3.75, λ*7=5.25, λ*9=6.75, etc. From simulation, we had A_1=1.966, then B_2=2+ (2.25-1.966)^2=2+0.284^2=2+0.0807=2.0807. Then A_2 = 2.25 + sqrt(4-2.0807)=2.25+ sqrt(1.9193)=2.25+1.385=3.635. Then λ*5=3.75, so D_2=0.115. Then B_3 = 4 + (3.75-3.635)^2=4+0.115^2=4+0.0132=4.0132. Then A_3 = 3.75 + sqrt(6-4.0132)=3.75+ sqrt(1.9868)=3.75+1.409=5.159. Then λ*7=5.25, so D_3=0.091. Then B_4 = 6 + (5.25-5.159)^2=6+0.091^2=6+0.0083=6.0083. Then A_4 = 5.25 + sqrt(8-6.0083)=5.25+ sqrt(1.9917)=5.25+1.411=6.661. Then λ*9=6.75, D_4=0.089. Then B_5 = 8 + (6.75-6.661)^2=8+0.089^2=8+0.0079=8.0079. Then A_5 = 6.75 + sqrt(10-8.0079)=6.75+ sqrt(1.9921)=6.75+1.411=8.161. Then λ*11=8.25, D_5=0.089. So D_k seems to be decreasing slowly. This suggests that if Alice plays x=0 on odd turns, she might be able to survive indefinitely because D_k stays positive and small. In the above, we have D_2=0.115, D_3=0.091, D_4=0.089, D_5=0.089, etc. It seems to converge to a small positive number. So for λ=0.75, Alice can survive by always playing 0 on odd turns, and then Bazza will always play the maximum x, but the linear sum will stay below the bound. So the game can be drawn. Thus, λ=0.75 is a draw.

But earlier we thought that for λ=0.7, Bazza can win. Let's test λ=0.7 with a similar strategy: set x=0 on odd turns. Then compute the recurrence.

This seems to continue, with S always increasing by about 1.414 each even turn, and T staying at the even bound (which is increasing by 2 each even turn, but we are not increasing T on odd turns). So T_odd = 2k, which is less than the next even bound? Actually, at odd turn n=2k+1, T_{2k+1} = 2k? Wait, in our simulation, we set x=0 on odd turns, so T after Alice's move is the previous even bound? Let's track: After Bazza's move at even 2k, T_{2k}=2k. Then Alice at odd 2k+1 sets x=0, so T_{2k+1}=2k (since no increase). Then Bazza at even 2k+2 chooses x_{2k+2}= sqrt(2k+2 - T_{2k+1}) = sqrt(2k+2 - 2k)= sqrt(2) ≈1.414, so T_{2k+2}=2k+2. So indeed, T_{odd} = 2k, which is less than the next even bound (2k+2) by 2. So the condition for Alice to survive at odd turn is that S_{2k+1} ≤ λ(2k+1). In our simulation, S_{2k+1} = S_{2k} (since x=0). And S_{2k} after Bazza's move at even 2k is A_k. So we need A_k ≤ λ(2k+1). In our simulation, we have A_k = S_{2k} and we computed S_{2k} values. For λ=0.75, we have S2=1.966, S4=3.635, S6=5.159, S8=6.661, S10=8.161, S12=9.319? Actually, from the pattern, we had S6≈5.159? Wait, we computed S6=5.159? In our earlier simulation for λ=0.75 with x3=0, x5=0, we got S4=3.503, S6=4.757? Let's recalc: For λ=0.75, we had S2=1.966, then we set x3=0, so S3=2.25, T3=2. Then Bazza at n=4: x4= sqrt(4-2)=1.414, S4=3.635? Actually, careful: S3 = λ*3 = 2.25 (since we set x3 = λ*3 - S2 = 2.25-1.966=0.284? Wait, we used x3=0? But we are considering the strategy where Alice sets x=0 on odd turns. That is not maximal; she is playing submaximal. So we need to compute the recurrence for that strategy.

In the strategy where Alice always sets x=0 on odd turns, she is not playing maximal; she is playing 0. Then S_{2k-1} = S_{2k-2} (since x=0). And T_{2k-1}=T_{2k-2}=2k-2? Actually, after Bazza's move at even 2k-2, T_{2k-2}=2k-2. Then Alice sets x=0, so S_{2k-1}=S_{2k-2} and T_{2k-1}=2k-2. Then Bazza at even 2k-1? Wait, turns: n=2k-2 (even) -> Bazza's move, then n=2k-1 (odd) -> Alice's move (x=0), then n=2k (even) -> Bazza's move. So after Alice's move at odd 2k-1, we have S_{2k-1}=S_{2k-2} and T_{2k-1}=2k-2. Then Bazza at even 2k chooses x_{2k} = sqrt(2k - T_{2k-1}) = sqrt(2k - (2k-2)) = sqrt(2) ≈1.414. So S_{2k}=S_{2k-2}+ sqrt(2). And T_{2k}=2k. So we have recurrence for S: S_2 = λ + sqrt(2-λ^2) (since first Bazza's move at n=2). Then for k≥2, S_{2k}=S_{2k-2}+ sqrt(2). So S_{2k} = λ + sqrt(2-λ^2) + (k-1)*sqrt(2). For λ=0.75, λ+ sqrt(2-0.5625)=0.75+ sqrt(1.4375)=0.75+1.199=1.949. Then S_4=1.949+1.414=3.363, S_6=3.363+1.414=4.777, S_8=4.777+1.414=6.191, S_10=6.191+1.414=7.605, etc. So S_{2k} ≈ 1.949 + (k-1)*1.414. Then Alice's odd turn condition: at turn 2k+1, she needs S_{2k+1} = S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1). For k=1, S_2=1.949, λ*3=2.25, ok. For k=2, S_4=3.363, λ*5=3.75, ok. For k=3, S_6=4.777, λ*7=5.25, ok. For k=4, S_8=6.191, λ*9=6.75, ok. For k=5, S_10=7.605, λ*11=8.25, ok. So this strategy allows Alice to survive indefinitely because S_{2k} is always less than λ(2k+1) for λ=0.75. Let's analyze the game more systematically. The game is symmetric in a sense; we can consider the "value" of the state. For the game to be a draw, there must exist a strategy for Alice that allows her to survive indefinitely, and also a strategy for Bazza that allows him to survive indefinitely. We can try to find the condition for Alice to have a winning strategy.

Define f(n) = S_n^2 - λ^2 n^2 + T_n - n. At the start, S_1=λ, T_1=λ^2, so f(1)=λ^2 - λ^2 + λ^2 -1 = λ^2 -1. Then after Bazza's move at n=2, we have S_2=λ+√(2-λ^2), T_2=2, so f(2)= (λ+√(2-λ^2))^2 - 4λ^2 + 2-2 = (λ^2+2λ√(2-λ^2)+2-λ^2) -4λ^2 = 2λ√(2-λ^2)+2 -4λ^2. This is messy.

Another approach: Use the concept of "strategy stealing" and "pairing". The game can be seen as a "race" between the linear sum and the square sum. The condition for the game to be drawn is that there exists a "balanced" sequence of moves such that the linear sum never exceeds λ n for odd n, and the square sum never exceeds n for even n. We can find the maximum possible λ for which such a balanced sequence exists, and then the answer is that Alice wins for λ > that value, Bazza wins for λ < that value, and the game is a draw for λ = that value.

Let's attempt to find the "critical" λ by analyzing the recurrence for the optimal play. Define a_k = S_{2k} (linear sum after Bazza's move at even 2k), and b_k = T_{2k-1} (square sum after Alice's move at odd 2k-1). The recurrence for optimal play (Alice tries to maximize her chance of winning, Bazza tries to maximize his) can be derived using the fact that each player will choose the best response given the other's previous moves. However, we can consider the "minimax" value.

From the necessary condition we derived earlier, if the game goes on forever, then λ ≥ 1. This is because for large n, we must have S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1) and also by Cauchy-Schwarz, S_{2k}^2 ≤ (2k) T_{2k} ≤ (2k)(2k) = 4k^2, so S_{2k} ≤ 2k. As k→∞, λ(2k+1) ≥ 2k implies λ ≥ 1. So if λ < 1, it's impossible for the game to continue indefinitely. Hence, for λ < 1, one of the players will eventually be unable to move. So for λ < 1, either Alice or Bazza has a winning strategy. For λ ≥ 1, we have the necessary condition for a draw; but we need to see if it's sufficient. For λ = 1, we already found a winning strategy for Alice (by playing x1=1, x3=0, x5=2, etc.). So for λ = 1, Alice wins. For λ > 1, we also found that Alice wins. So the answer is that Alice has a winning strategy for all λ ≥ 1, and Bazza has a winning strategy for λ < 1?
